  "sections": {
    "__preamble__": "DEFENCE STUDIES\nDefence Studies\nRoutledge Taylor &amp; Francis Group\nISSN: 1470-2436 (Print) 1743-9698 (Online) Journal homepage: www.tandfonline.com/journals/fdef20",
    "## NATO's institutional adaptation": "Explanations of NATO's evolving post-cold war role can be divided into three main theoretical camps. Realist and neorealist scholars have emphasised the continuity of external threats and have viewed the perpetuation of NATO as being based on the US desire to control and influence European members of the alliance . Institutionalist scholars have emphasised the adaptability of NATO to new security challenges and emphasised such concepts as path dependence and institutional “stickiness” as explaining NATO's perpetuation, which is to say that the adaptation of NATO structures has been seen as preferable to the reinvention/reestablishment of the alliance . Constructivist scholars have focused more on NATO's common identity as a community of democracies and the desire to expand that community to Central and Eastern Europe . Arguably, all three of these approaches are of utility in expanding NATO's emerging cyber security role. NATO members have consistently emphasised the threat from cyber attacks, and that the threat comes from state and state-supported actors (realism); there has been a concerted effort to adapt NATO's institutions, operations and doctrines to respond to cyber attacks (institutionalism); and cyber attacks cannot be separated from identity politics, even when the identity of cyber attackers is hard to establish (constructivism).\nThis section of the article combines insights from realist and institutional approaches to alliance transformation and argues that cyber threats have driven institutional change within NATO. In this respect, NATO has been under pressure to adjust its functions and design to deal with changing environmental demands . This process of evolution has taken place slowly and incrementally and has been only one facet of a broader adaptation on the part of the alliance to a diversifying and globalising security environment. It should also be noted that institutional adaptation is not a guarantee of effectiveness . Nevertheless, NATO's institutional strength and adaptability has made it a useful tool for dialogue, policy development and decision-making on cyber security within the Euro Atlantic area, and created opportunities for developing unified positions on the core issues.\nMore specifically, there appear to have been four drivers of this process of institutional evolution. First, NATO has taken steps to enhance its cyber security due to its operational experiences, including in Kosovo in 1999, Afghanistan from 2003 and Libya in 2011. These conflicts have taken on a cyber dimension and NATO has had to incorporate cyber into strategic planning as a result. Second, the events of 9/11 created serious concerns within NATO over cyber terrorism (and terrorist use of the internet more broadly) and this has driven NATO's cyber security focus. Third, geostrategic rivalries with Russia and China have taken on an increasingly online dimension and NATO has responded accordingly. Fourth, the volume and severity of cyber attacks against NATO itself and its members has increased rapidly in the last decade. These changing dynamics have given NATO's political leaders incentives to enhance NATO's cyber security capabilities and created “triggers” for institutional change .\nCyber security first appeared on NATO's formal agenda at the 2002 Prague Summit, where NATO members committed to the “Initiation of measures to strengthen defence against cyber attacks” . This new focus stemmed from the conflict in Kosovo in 1999, where pro-Serbian hackers had targeted NATO communications infrastructure with a view to affecting its war fighting capabilities. A NATO website responsible for promoting NATO's role in the conflict was subjected to sustained DDoS attacks from Serbian hackers, and Chinese hackers targeted NATO members in retaliation for the accidental bombing of the Chinese Embassy in Belgrade, leading to the White House website being taken offline for three days . The summit highlighted NATO's “ability to adjust to new priorities and to adapt its capabilities in order to meet new challenges” . This process of change was described as part of an “evolution” and was to occur “incrementally”, “on a continual and deliberate basis” . In a more concrete sense, the 2002 summit established an institutional capacity to respond to this growing concern. A NATO Computer Incident Response Capability (NCIRC) was formed and the NATO Cyber Defence Programme was established.\nNATO also took on a more active role in the fight against terrorism at this time. . The new commitment to cyber defence at the Prague summit was referred to as being part of a “package of measures ... to strengthen NATO's capabilities to defend against terrorism”  and was endorsed when the alliance was grappling with its response to 9/11, the US intervention in Afghanistan, in which several NATO members took an early lead role, and in the context of a changing US national security strategy that placed counterterrorism at its core. The US government was also instituting domestic reforms at this time that influenced the NATO process. In October 2001, the Bush administration signed the Patriot Act into law, which enhanced the government's domestic and foreign surveillance powers, established the National Electronic Crime Task Force, extended government powers to intercept and access electronic communications, and enhanced America's digital forensics capabilities. Perhaps most significantly, the Act included provisions for classifying computer crimes as acts of terrorism for the first time . Further to this, the Bush administration created the Office of Cyberspace Security in the White House and appointed prominent counterterrorism coordinator, Richard Clarke, to lead it. The newly created Department for Homeland Security was also given a statutory cyber security role. Tom Ridge, its first Director, highlighted the extent of the US government's concerns at this time: “Terrorists can sit at one computer connected to one network and can create worldwide havoc. [They] don't necessarily need a bomb or explosives to cripple a\nsector of the economy or shut down a power grid” (2003 cited Weimann 2004). This may have been an example of cyber security hyperbole, but the fear created by 9/11 and the prospect of terrorist groups developing sophisticated cyber attack capabilities were significant drivers of the US government's response to cyber security issues in the early post-9/11 period and influenced NATO's growing role in this area of policy.\nThe NATO summit in Riga in 2006 further endorsed the need to continue efforts to enhance NATO's cyber security and emphasis was placed on securing NATO's own information systems against cyber attack . Arguably, however, a broader NATO role in the cyber security of its members was still on the backburner. The 2007 cyber attacks against Estonia, however, elevated the issue of cyber security within the alliance and shifted NATO's focus away from cyber terrorism to a focus on the threats posed by nation states and state-sponsored hackers. The attacks were thought to be a response to the removal of a monument to the Soviet liberation of Estonia, and took place over a sustained period of three weeks. All of the Estonian government's websites were shut down, along with the sites of two major banks, a number of political parties and three of the country's biggest news media organisations. From a strategic point of view, the attacks demonstrated a number of the problems outlined in the previous section, particularly the issue of attribution. The Estonian government accused the Russian government of instigating the attacks and saw Russia's refusal to participate in official investigations as confirming a level of culpability . NATO officials strongly suspected Russian state involvement, but found no direct evidence of a link. One NATO spokesperson said, “I won't point fingers. But these were not things done by a few individuals. This clearly bore the hallmarks of something concerted” (2007 cited Traynor 2007). The Estonian attacks also prompted a vigorous and prolonged internal debate within the alliance as to whether a cyber attack could form the basis of an Article 5 commitment. Estonia's defence Minister acknowledged that,“At present, NATO does not define cyber-attacks as a clear military action. This means that the provisions of Article 5 of the North Atlantic Treaty, or, in other words collective self-defence, will not automatically be extended to the attacked country. (2007 cited Traynor 2007)”\nHowever, the sustained cyber assault was widely viewed as a violation of Estonia's sovereignty and even an encroachment on its territorial integrity. As Linnar Viik, a consultant to the Estonian government, asserted, “... this is not some virtual world. This is part of our independence. And these attacks were an attempt to take one country back to the cave, back to the Stone Age” (2007 cited Finn 2007). Estonian Prime Minister Andrus Ansip asked, “What's the difference between a blockade of harbours or airports of sovereign states and the blockade of government institutions and newspaper websites?” (2007 cited Rid 2012b). NATO also recognised the severity of the problem, with one Brussels-based official stating: “This is an operational security issue, something we're taking very seriously. It goes to the heart of the alliance's modus operandi” (2007 cited Traynor 2007). In 2007, however, NATO was some way from establishing whether cyber attacks constituted an “armed attack” as stipulated in Article 5, and implementing a collective response to the attacks was problematic given the difficulty of attribution. Nevertheless, the incident formed the basis of a more unified alliance approach to cyber security and the development of enhanced capabilities .\nThe cyber attacks against Estonia in 2007 were not an isolated example of the evolving cyber threat. A number of other serious incidents raised the profile of cyber\nsecurity within NATO member states in late 2007 and 2008 and fuelled attention to strategic vulnerabilities within the transatlantic community. In August 2007, it was reported that Chinese hackers had broken into the networks of the German Chancellery and three other Berlin Ministries, and in November 2007 British intelligence agency MI5 stated that the PLA had been conducting a concerted campaign of cyber espionage against British businesses. In June 2008, Lithuania faced a barrage of cyber attacks after it vetoed an EU energy partnership deal with Russia. The Russian war against Georgia in August 2008 also featured Russian-based hackers using cyber attacks as a precursor to the invasion in order to create confusion among Georgian military units and government ministries and to prevent a coordinated response to the assault . Lastly, in November 2008, the US Pentagon issued a ban on all external hard drives after a USB drive was used by a foreign intelligence agency to upload spyware and data exfiltration software to Defence Department computers .\nThis growth in malicious cyber activity in 2007--2008 created a window of opportunity to develop multilateral mechanisms to respond to cyber security threats and various such measures were put in place over the course of 2008. In January that year, as a direct result of the Estonia attacks, NATO members endorsed a new Cyber Defence Policy, and in March the North Atlantic Council (NAC) established the Cyber Defence Management Authority (CDMA). The CDMA aimed to integrate the alliance's cyber security functions and provide support for NATO members in the event of cyber attacks. A NATO spokesperson described the role of the centre as, “defence, whether an attack comes from state, criminal or other sources” (2008 cited Grant 2008). Later in 2008, the NATO Cooperative Cyber Defence Centre of Excellence (CCD COE) was established in Tallinn, Estonia. While not part of the formal NATO command structure, the centre has taken a role in advancing NATO cyber security doctrine policy and developing cyber security capabilities. It has also helped to reduce alliance-wide transaction costs through the NATO “Smart Defence” initiative. Wallander (2000) has explained how highly institutionalised alliances like NATO create “assets” which help to sustain the alliance even as the security environment changes. The formation of these bodies should be seen in this context. They have provided mechanisms for the pooling of resources, avenues for negotiations between NATO members on cyber issues, enhanced decision-making within the alliance, and created incentives for all members to develop multilateral approaches to cyber security rather than isolated national ones.\nThe next major institutional development for NATO was the inclusion of cyber security in the November 2010 Strategic Concept, the third such Concept to be released in the post-cold war era. This was another important milestone for the alliance and a firm indication that NATO members considered cyber security to be an important area of focus. The concept emphasised “the next phase in NATO's evolution” and the need for “continuous reform” to meet modern security challenges . On cyber security specifically, it stated that cyber attacks were not just a national problem, but a collective threat to the “prosperity, security, and stability” of the whole of the Euro Atlantic area, recognising that such attacks were becoming, “more frequent, more organised and more costly in the damage that they inflict on government administrations, businesses, economies and potentially also transportation and supply networks and other critical infrastructure” . Interestingly, while the concept made reference to NATO's “ability to prevent, detect, defend against and recover from cyber-attacks” , the notion of deterrence was notably absent.\nNATO's institutional adaption to the threat of cyber attacks continued with the adoption of a revised Cyber Defence Policy in 2011, which placed emphasis on minimum\nrequirements for cyber defence of national networks and an integrated system of cyber governance within NATO, with the North Atlantic Council as the ultimate driver of NATO policy and cooperation in this area. The revised policy was another landmark in NATO's strategic thinking on cyber security, highlighted the need for pragmatic responses to cyber attacks (not being constrained by Article 5, for example), and recognised the importance of prevention of and resilience to attacks . The establishment of a Rapid Reaction Team in 2012 was also noteworthy. A team of six NATO computer experts were tasked with responding to cyber attacks in real time and re-establishing computer network functionality in the event of cyber attacks and/or network exploitation.\nA further notable contribution to NATO's adaptation to cyber security challenges was the publication of The Tallinn Manual on the International Law Applicable to Cyber Warfare in March 2013. This document was produced by team of legal and technical experts working in conjunction with the CCD COE and aimed to codify and clarify possible responses to both the legal and strategic dilemmas around cyber attacks by apply existing international law to the cyber security arena. The document has advanced NATO's legal and strategic thinking on key cyber security issues, including on the problem of attribution, where it argues that states should bear some responsibility for cyber attacks emanating from within their jurisdiction. There are areas, however, that are underexplored in the document, including legal prohibitions on attacks that do not constitute a use of armed force and which take place outside of armed conflict (which still constitute the vast majority of cyber attacks), and kinetic-to-cyber operations, where conventional force might be used to destroy digital infrastructure .\nFinally, in September 2014, at its summit in Cardiff, NATO members endorsed the enhanced Cyber Defence Policy. In many ways, this was the culmination of the policy debate that was started within NATO by the attacks against Estonia in 2007. As a result of the policy, NATO will consider (and potentially implement) a collective Article 5 response to cyber attacks against NATO members, just as it did in response to the terrorist attacks on 9/11. This landmark in NATO's cyber security evolution is not without its problems. Uncertainty remains as to how a collective response can be implemented when attribution is problematic, and it is not clear what threshold a cyber attack would need to meet to trigger an Article 5 response. Minor intrusions into NATO networks are clearly not what NATO policy-makers have in mind. However, cyber attacks with a severe impact on the economy, critical infrastructure and national security of NATO members may well fall into this category. As Jamie Shea, head of NATO's Emerging Security Challenges division, has stated: “We are keeping that ambiguous so a potential aggressor does not get the idea they can carry out cyber attacks up to a certain level with impunity” (2014 cited Washford 2014). It is also unclear what a collective cyber response by NATO would actually look like. NATO does not have the authority to implement sanctions against states for cyber attacks; digital retaliation is difficult although not impossible, as can be seen in the case of the (still unclaimed) attacks against North Korea in response to the Sony hack; and an attack using conventional force, which has not been ruled out, would clearly need to be proportional to the original offence. Nevertheless, 15 years after NATO first experienced cyber attacks, the enhanced Cyber Defence Policy had brought cyber security firmly inside the purview of NATO's evolving responsibilities. It has indicated that the alliance will respond deliberately and proportionally to any significant attack depending on the extent of the damage, the level of attribution, and the motives and identity of the attackers  (Table 2).\nDefence Studies 309\n# NATO's cyber security doctrine\nAlthough this institutional change has occurred incrementally, and has been driven by a diverse set of events and actors, cyber security appears to have found a foothold in NATO's doctrine for the defence of its members and for the general employment of NATO power in the post-cold war era. But what do these institutional developments tell us about NATO's collective cyber security doctrine? How do these capabilities, policies and institutions actually shape how NATO acts in cyberspace, and do they constitute a unified or uniform approach to cyber security across the transatlantic region?\nNATO's first priority has always been to defend the North Atlantic area, and, even though cyber threats emanate from cyberspace, the alliance's doctrine is still, for the most part, focused on the protection of its own computer networks. NATO's cyber command is centrally located in Brussels, where the CDMA and NCIRC provide coordination, real-time monitoring capabilities and intelligence sharing functions , and alliance officials are aware of the need to protect NATO's digital infrastructure spread across its many international sites. While NATO has taken a broader role in cyber defence as a result of the recent adoption of the enhanced Cyber Defence Policy, its own operational security is paramount.\nSecond, NATO's cyber security doctrine and posture is still largely defensive. The alliance has not been involved in developing offensive cyber attack capabilities. These sorts of capabilities remain the purview of the national intelligence and security institutions of individual members. It may be that NATO's political and military command would be informed if a NATO member was involved in conducting offensive cyber security operations, especially if it was part of a wider NATO mission, but at the present time NATO's cyber command is not envisaged as having such a role.\nThird, although deterring cyber attacks is clearly not as straight forward as deterring conventional attacks from state actors, NATO cyber doctrine appears to be based in deterrence by denial. Given the difficulty of retaliating against cyber attackers and the inability to compel attackers to desist from attacks, denying them the ability to achieve their objectives has become the focus. NATO will make it as difficult as possible to infiltrate its systems and make sure hackers are aware that they are unlikely to be able to achieve their intended effects (Healey and van Bochoven 2012). Jamie Shea has recently highlighted the utility of this defensive posture .\nTable 2. The institutional development of NATO's cyber security role.\n|  1999 | Cyber attacks directed against NATO during the Kosovo conflict  |\n| --- | --- |\n|  2002 | Prague summit – Cyber security appears on NATO’s formal agenda for the first time. Computer Incident Response Capability (NCIRC) formed. Cyber Defence Programme established  |\n|  2006 | Riga Summit – Emphasis placed on the security of NATO’s own information systems  |\n|  2007 | Cyber attacks against Estonia focus attention within NATO on the strategic issues involved  |\n|  2008 | NATO Cyber Defence Policy approved by the NAC, Cyber Defence Management Authority (CDMA) created, NATO Cooperative Cyber Defence Centre of Excellence (CCD COE) established  |\n|  2010 | Cyber Security appears in NATO’s 2010 Strategic Concept and forms one of the pillars of the NATO Emerging Security Challenges Division  |\n|  2011 | Revised Policy on Cyber Defence Adopted – emphasis on defence rather than deterrence  |\n|  2012 | Rapid Reaction Team (RRT) becomes operational  |\n|  2013 | Tallinn Manual on the International Law Applicable to Cyber Warfare released  |\n|  2014 | NATO adopts an enhanced Cyber Defence Policy through which cyber attacks become part of NATO’s Article 5 outlook  |\nFourth, NATO's cyber security posture is now based primarily on states and state-sponsored hackers. While NATO has clearly been influenced by the diffusion of power and influence away from the state in the post-cold war era, and particularly threats from terrorist groups and non-affiliated hackers, concerns about Russia and China have contributed to a revised sense of state adversaries during this period. Despite NATO's preponderance of power in other areas, its strategic rivals have proved highly capable in the cyber security arena. General Keith Alexander, head of the US Cyber Command, has described Russia as a “near peer” in terms of its cyber capabilities (2010 cited Giles 2011) and Giles (2011, p. 54) has argued both China's and Russia's emphasis on semi-autonomous agencies in cyber security operations gives them a distinct advantage over the US and its allies. Russian and Chinese-based hackers have regularly targeted NATO's networks in response to political disputes, most recently in response to NATO's stance on the Ukraine crisis , and both countries have an interest in exfiltrating the confidential, operational, strategic and political information that flows through NATO networks. Although some attempts have been made to enhance cyber security cooperation between NATO and Russia, such as dialogue at the NATO-Russia Council level , the Russian annexation of Crimea and on-going efforts to destabilise eastern Ukraine, which have taken place alongside new cyber attacks against NATO members, have put any meaningful cooperation on cyber issues on hold. The US Justice Department's indictment of five member of the PLA in May 2014 has similarly stalled any attempt to cooperate with China on cyber issues .\nLastly, as with many other areas of NATO's security doctrine, NATO's approach to cyber security is based around developing cooperative relationships, both internally and externally. NATO has developed a range of formal partnerships in the post-cold war era that are increasingly addressing cyber security issues, including the Istanbul Cooperation Initiative (ICI), the Mediterranean Dialogue (MD) and the Euro-Atlantic Partnership Council (EAPC). Cyber Security is increasingly a component of the Individual Partnership Action Plans (IPAPs) negotiated with countries around the world, such as New Zealand, and NATO has developed enhanced links with the private sector and critical infrastructure providers in advancing cyber defence in the North Atlantic area . These diverse networks of partnerships allow for global cooperation on cyber security issues and help to develop cyber security norms, standards and capabilities. In this respect, NATO's cyber security doctrine covers and is part of each of its three main operational pillars: Crisis Management (e.g. the crisis surrounding the cyber attacks against Estonian in 2007), Collective Defence (e.g. the enhanced Cyber Defence Policy) and Cooperative Security.",
    "## A transatlantic divide?": "NATO has frequently been beset by intra-alliance tensions and in the cyber security arena there appears to be at least some evidence of a divergence in European and US policy approaches. Recent revelations by former NSA contractor, Edward Snowden, for example, have exposed transatlantic tensions around surveillance, and the alleged NSA hacking of Chancellor Angela Merkel's cell phone, which led to an angry phone call between Obama and Merkel and the summoning of the US Ambassador to Germany, was a major embarrassment for the US government . The US has arguably taken a harder security stance on cyber issues since 9/11 because of its desire to protect the US homeland, and because it faces increased strategic rivalry from rival global\npower centres. A more aggressive cyber posture can certainly be seen in the establishment of the US Cyber Command, a unified and expanding military command structure tasked with coordinating defensive and offensive cyber operations for all branches of the US military, the deployment of the Stuxnet software against Iranian nuclear centrifuges, the Patriot Act, and the mass surveillance programmes run by the NSA, including the Prism programme. The use of offensive and even pre-emptive operations by the US government has also grown in recent years, with a high number of ICT specialists employed to penetrate foreign networks . In 2012, Obama signed Presidential Decision Directive 20, a classified document that gave the US military the authority to conduct more aggressive cyber operations to thwart cyber attacks against US systems .\nThe wealth of cyber resources and capabilities that exist within the US military and associated agencies may be a reason in and of itself why the US approach to managing cyber conflict has been dominated by that branch of the US government. Mary O Connell takes up this line of argument, suggesting that the US government has become “trapped in an ideology of militarism”  and claiming that “the US military today has the largest concentration of expertise and legal authority with respect to cyberspace” . She further notes that in 2009, following the Estonia attacks, the Russia Georgia war and the release of the Stuxnet virus, the US government “began releasing a number of policies on cyber security that were predominantly military in orientation” . This would seem to support the line of argument advanced by this article, that threats from state rather than non-state actors explain the elevation of cyber security as an issue within the transatlantic security community.\nThis hard power approach to cyber security in the US could also be explained by the rhetoric being used to describe cyber attacks in the American context. Former Defence Secretary Leon Panetta's claim that the US could face a “cyber Pearl Harbour” is a prime example of the securitisation and militarisation of cyber discourse. When there is a propensity at the highest levels of government to adopt Second World War and cold war analogies to characterise cyber conflict, this can shape US policy and doctrine in a certain direction. Shachtman and Singer (2011) argue that clear links can be found between cold war strategic thinking and the formulation of current US policy, especially the idea that cyber attacks might be met with conventional attacks; an idea “lifted” from the 1960s strategy of flexible response. Commercial and political motives might also have led to the US taking a more military-based approach to cyber security issues. They further argue that “there is a massive amount of threat inflation going on in Washington's discussion of online dangers, most frequently by those with political or profit motives in hyping the threats” Shachtman and Singer (2011). While we should be careful in suggesting that a new “cyber industrial complex” is forming in America, the huge US government spending on cyber protection for the federal government, such as a $1 billion contract awarded to Raytheon by the Department for Homeland Security in 2015, may create incentives for responses to cyber security threats to be militarised. Equally, threat inflation may displace attention from the arguably more pervasive problem of cyber crime.\nIn Europe, at least on the surface, a softer cyber security approach is evident. EU responses to cyber security have typically emphasised criminal and civil procedures for dealing with cyber attacks, reflecting a European preference for soft power over hard power, and there has been a broader reluctance in Europe to militarise cyberspace. The new European Union Cyber Security Strategy, for example, is based on promoting\nnorms in cyberspace, encouraging dialogue between nations, enhancing technical capacity and resilience, and fighting cybercrime . The most controversial aspect has been the requirement for businesses in Europe, and particularly critical infrastructure providers, to report cyber attacks to a central authority. Overall though, the strategy is defensively orientated and non-militaristic.\nThe level of transatlantic divergence on cyber security issues should not be overstated, however. Given the impact of 9/11 on the US national security establishment, it is not surprising that the balance in America has tilted towards security over privacy in the decade or so since the attacks. In the context of the rise of China, the US pivot to Asia and an increasingly assertive Russia, the US is also bound to have an interest in staying ahead of its state competitors in cyber space. The US has shown some restraint in the use of its cyber capabilities and a recognisable commitment to the emergence of normative constraints on cyber warfare. Offensive cyber attacks were considered by the Obama administration as part of the campaign against Colonel Gadhafi's Libya in 2011, but a reluctance to set a dangerous precedent in how wars are fought, as well as a degree of concern over securing Congressional support for such strategy, meant that the campaign was limited to conventional capabilities . The Obama administration has also announced plans to end mass surveillance programmes and has stated that the US will cease monitoring the personal communications of European leaders.\nWithin Europe there are also certain states that are moving closer to the US in developing harder cyber security capabilities, and doing so outside of the NATO framework. The UK government, for example, has taken steps to merge cyber security intelligence and military operations, and the French and German militaries are both developing offensive cyber capabilities . As previously stated, while there has been no NATO-led process for offensive cyber security, NATO's leading powers, including the US, are pursuing similar strategies domestically.\nCommon interests, values and threats also continue to bind American and European approaches to cyber security together. Both sides of the Atlantic advocate an open and accessible global cyber commons with minimal government restrictions, and an open internet is seen by both sides as central to driving economic growth in the wake of the 2008 financial crisis. The Americans and Europeans are also unified in their approach to fighting cyber crime and securing critical infrastructure . This general approach stands in opposition to Russia and China, who are arguably more concerned with being able to control information that could destabilise their societies and who have argued for international rights to restrict and censor internet traffic on that basis . While this divide may be hindering wider international cooperation, it is evidence of a level of unity on the basic issues within the transatlantic area.\nIntra-alliance management of cyber security issues also remains an important area of focus for the NATO allies, not least because some NATO members are clearly more concerned about cyber security than others. Estonia, the NATO member victim to the most significant attacks on its critical infrastructure, does not have the same level of cyber security concerns as Portugal, for example. But this is not necessarily an impediment to a NATO cyber security role, especially as NATO members have tended to specialise and develop niche capabilities that benefit the wider membership. Perhaps now more than ever NATO's capability aggregation role -- amassing capabilities against a common threat -- and its role in facilitating “smart defence”, are of crucial importance . Former Secretary General Rasmussen emphasised this himself in 2011, saying that “Smart defence is about building security for less money by working\ntogether and being more flexible” . In an environment where NATO members' defence budgets are under strain, and where the US is concerned about a lack of European Defence spending, specialisation within the alliance will be increasingly important. NATO's Cyber Security Centre of Excellence is an example of this concept in practice, where some NATO members (Estonia, Germany, Italy, Latvia, Lithuania, Slovakia and Spain) have invested in a valuable institutional resource that is beneficial to all members. Defence cooperation creates efficiencies and lowers transaction costs; this is being achieved through NATO's institutional frameworks and potentially lowering the scope for intra-alliance divides.",
    "## Conclusion": "While NATO has tried to come to terms with some of the strategic challenges involved in enhancing the cyber security of its members, there are still some unresolved and perhaps unresolvable cyber security issues. Reliably attributing cyber attacks to specific actors, responding to the diversity of actors involved and deterring cyber attacks, for example, will continue to be difficult and possibly intractable problems. NATO appears so far to have accepted this and adopted a cyber security posture that is pragmatic and deals with attacks on a case by case basis. Its defensive role, based around deterrence by denial, is appropriate given the reluctance to use the alliance for offensive cyber purposes, and its focus is in areas where it has a clear and compelling political and strategic interest; in cyber terrorism, cyber espionage and cyber warfare.\nThe defence of NATO's own digital networks continues to be of paramount importance. However, there is some evidence that the alliance is taking a wider and deeper role in cyber security in the North Atlantic area. The adoption of an Article 5 approach to cyber attacks is foremost in this regard, but the efforts that have been made to forge private sector partnerships and to develop normative and legal mechanisms for cyber security are also significant. Ultimately, cyber security remains a national responsibility for NATO members.\nWhat is perhaps most remarkable about how NATO has adapted to cyber security challenges is the institutional capacity the alliance has put in place. This institutional development has been driven mostly by external threats, from terrorist groups initially, but most recently by state actors, and these have accelerated the political and institutional focus on cyber security within the alliance. The alliance's integrated military command has always been one of its most important institutional assets and has historically allowed NATO members to coordinate military operations. The political integration and coordination provided by the North Atlantic Council has also been noteworthy throughout alliance history. In a similar vein, NATO now has a common cyber security capacity through which members can coordinate responses to cyber security incidents and provide channels of communication that are likely to continue to be important as cyber threats evolve.\nTransatlantic tensions on cyber security issues are certainly in evidence, and these stem from broader tensions between the US and Europe over the response to 9/11 and the role of the internet in surveillance. Despite this, there is a considerable degree of unity on the core cyber security challenges within the NATO membership. The balance between privacy and security will continue to be debated within NATO member states, but at this stage, this does not appear to be inhibiting a common and unified approach to at least defensive cyber operations. Whether NATO will take on an offensive cyber security posture at some stage will be an issue to monitor. The requirements of\nimplementing an Article 5 response to a major cyber attack might provide momentum for the development of offensive capabilities to be deployed as part of a unified NATO command, but it is more likely that NATO's most prominent players will continue to develop and deploy these kinds of capabilities outside of NATO channels.\nFinally, it is worth returning to NATO's core functions: collective defence, crisis management and cooperative security. In all these areas, a NATO cyber security role would appear to have considerable merit. Global security challenges invariably require multilateral solutions and NATO has a range of adaptable institutional capabilities that are reasonably well suited to the task at hand.",
    "__postscript__": "## Disclosure statement\nNo potential conflict of interest was reported by the author."
